<li><a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stiglitz" title="Joseph Stiglitz">Joseph Stiglitz</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Thomas_J._Sargent" title="Thomas J. Sargent">Thomas J. Sargent</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Paul_Krugman" title="Paul Krugman">Paul Krugman</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Greg_Mankiw" title="Greg Mankiw">N. Gregory Mankiw</a></li></ul>
</div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">
<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background:transparent;border-top:1px solid #aaa;text-align:center;;color: var(--color-base)">See also</div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content hlist">

</div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below plainlist">
<ul><li><span class="nowrap"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Emblem-money.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Emblem-money.svg/20px-Emblem-money.svg.png" decoding="async" width="15" height="15" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Emblem-money.svg/40px-Emblem-money.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></a></span> </span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Economics" title="Portal:Economics">Economics&#32;portal</a></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Emblem-money.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Emblem-money.svg/20px-Emblem-money.svg.png" decoding="async" width="15" height="15" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Emblem-money.svg/40px-Emblem-money.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></a></span> </span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Business" title="Portal:Business">Business&#32;portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333133064" /><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239400231">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}}@media print{.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:none!important}}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Macroeconomics_sidebar" title="Template:Macroeconomics sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Macroeconomics_sidebar" title="Template talk:Macroeconomics sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Macroeconomics_sidebar" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Macroeconomics sidebar"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Gross domestic product</b> (<b>GDP</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Money" title="Money">monetary</a> <a href="/wiki/Measurement_in_economics" title="Measurement in economics">measure</a> of the total <a href="/wiki/Market_value" title="Market value">market value</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> of all of the <a href="/wiki/Final_good" title="Final good">final goods</a> and services which are produced and rendered during a specific period of time by a <a href="/wiki/Country" title="Country">country</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or countries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> GDP is often used to measure the economic activity of a country or region.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The major components of GDP are consumption, government spending, net exports (exports minus imports), and investment. Changing any of these factors can increase the size of the economy. For example, population growth through mass immigration can raise consumption and demand for public services, thereby contributing to GDP growth. However, GDP is not a measure of overall <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_living" title="Standard of living">standard of living</a> or well-being, as it does not account for how income is distributed among the population. A country may rank high in GDP but still experience <a href="/wiki/Jobless_growth" class="mw-redirect" title="Jobless growth">jobless growth</a> depending on its planned economic structure and strategies. Dividing total GDP by the population gives an idealized rough measure of <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(nominal)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (nominal) per capita">GDP per capita</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Several national and international economic organizations, such as the <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> and the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a>, maintain their own definitions of GDP.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>GDP is often used as a metric for <a href="/wiki/International_comparisons" title="International comparisons">international comparisons</a> as well as a <a href="/wiki/Broad_measures_of_economic_progress" title="Broad measures of economic progress">broad measure of economic progress</a>. It serves as a statistical indicator of national development and progress. Total GDP can also be broken down into the contribution of each industry or sector of the economy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Nominal GDP is useful when comparing national economies on the international market using current <a href="/wiki/Exchange_rate" title="Exchange rate">exchange rate</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To compare economies over time <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">inflation</a> can be adjusted by comparing <a href="/wiki/Real_and_nominal_value" title="Real and nominal value">real instead of nominal values</a>. For cross-country comparisons, GDP figures are often adjusted for differences in the <a href="/wiki/Cost_of_living" title="Cost of living">cost of living</a> using <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">purchasing power parity</a> (PPP). <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">GDP per capita at purchasing power parity</a> can be useful for comparing <a href="/wiki/Standard_of_living" title="Standard of living">living standards</a> between nations. 
</p><p>GDP has been criticized for leaving out key <a href="/wiki/Externality" title="Externality">externalities</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Resource_extraction" class="mw-redirect" title="Resource extraction">resource extraction</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2025)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Environmental_impact" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental impact">environmental impact</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unpaid_work" title="Unpaid work">unpaid domestic work</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Alternative economic indicators such as <a href="/wiki/Doughnut_(economic_model)" title="Doughnut (economic model)">doughnut economics</a> use other measures, such as the <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> or <a href="/wiki/OECD_Better_Life_Index" title="OECD Better Life Index">Better Life Index</a>, as better approaches to measuring the effect of the economy on <a href="/wiki/Human_development_(economics)" title="Human development (economics)">human development</a> and <a href="/wiki/Well-being" title="Well-being">well-being</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size skin-invert-image" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Quarterly_gross_domestic_product.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Quarterly_gross_domestic_product.png/330px-Quarterly_gross_domestic_product.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="120" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Quarterly_gross_domestic_product.png/500px-Quarterly_gross_domestic_product.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Quarterly_gross_domestic_product.png/960px-Quarterly_gross_domestic_product.png 2x" data-file-width="4837" data-file-height="1928" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">U.S.</a> <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/year-over-year" class="extiw" title="wikt:year-over-year">YoY</a> Quarterly gross domestic product growth rate</figcaption></figure>
<p>Sir <a href="/wiki/William_Petty" title="William Petty">William Petty</a> came up with a concept of GDP, to calculate the <a href="/wiki/Tax_burden" class="mw-redirect" title="Tax burden">tax burden</a>, and argue landlords were unfairly taxed during <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Dutch_Wars" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Dutch Wars">warfare between the Dutch and the English</a> between 1652 and 1674.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Charles_Davenant" title="Charles Davenant">Charles Davenant</a> developed the method further in 1695.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The modern concept of GDP was first developed by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Kuznets" title="Simon Kuznets">Simon Kuznets</a> for a 1934 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">U.S. Congress</a> report, where he warned against its use as a measure of welfare (see below under <i><a href="#Limitations_and_criticisms">limitations and criticisms</a></i>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-kuznets&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kuznets-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/Bretton_Woods_Conference" title="Bretton Woods Conference">Bretton Woods Conference</a> in 1944, GDP became the main tool for measuring a country's economy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> At that time <a href="/wiki/Gross_national_product" class="mw-redirect" title="Gross national product">gross national product</a> (GNP) was the preferred estimate, which differed from GDP in that it measured production by a country's citizens at home and abroad rather than its "resident institutional units" (see <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> definition above). The switch from GNP to GDP in the United States occurred in 1991. The role that measurements of GDP played in World War II was crucial to the subsequent political acceptance of GDP values as indicators of national development and progress.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A crucial role was played here by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Commerce" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Department of Commerce">U.S. Department of Commerce</a> under <a href="/wiki/Milton_Gilbert" title="Milton Gilbert">Milton Gilbert</a> where ideas from Kuznets were embedded into <a href="/wiki/Institution" title="Institution">institutions</a>.
</p><p>The history of the concept of GDP should be distinguished from the history of changes in many ways of estimating it. The value added by firms is relatively easy to calculate from their accounts, but the value added by the <a href="/wiki/Public_sector" title="Public sector">public sector</a>, by financial industries, and by <a href="/wiki/Intangible_asset" title="Intangible asset">intangible asset</a> creation is more complex. These activities are increasingly important in developed economies, and the international conventions governing their estimation and their inclusion or exclusion in GDP regularly change in an attempt to keep up with industrial advances. In the words of one academic economist, "The actual number for GDP is, therefore, the product of a vast patchwork of statistics and a complicated set of processes carried out on the raw data to fit them to the conceptual framework."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> officially adopted GDP in 1993 as its indicator of economic performance. Previously, China had relied on a Marxist-inspired national accounting system.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Determining_gross_domestic_product_(GDP)"><span id="Determining_gross_domestic_product_.28GDP.29"></span>Determining gross domestic product (GDP)</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Determining gross domestic product (GDP)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:How_is_GDP_calculated%3F.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/How_is_GDP_calculated%3F.png/330px-How_is_GDP_calculated%3F.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="881" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/How_is_GDP_calculated%3F.png/500px-How_is_GDP_calculated%3F.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/How_is_GDP_calculated%3F.png/900px-How_is_GDP_calculated%3F.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="2642" /></a><figcaption>An infographic explaining how GDP is calculated in the UK</figcaption></figure>
<p>GDP can be determined in three ways, all of which should, theoretically, give the same result. They are the production (or output or value added) approach, the income approach, and the speculated expenditure approach. It is representative of the total output and income within an economy.
</p><p>The most direct of the three is the production approach, which sums the outputs of every class of enterprise to arrive at the total. The expenditure approach works on the principle that all of the products must be bought by somebody, therefore the value of the total product must be equal to people's total expenditures in buying things. The income approach works on the principle that the incomes of the productive factors ("producers", colloquially) must be equal to the value of their product, and determines GDP by finding the sum of all producers' incomes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Production_approach">Production approach</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Production approach"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Also known as the Value Added Approach, it calculates how much value is contributed at each stage of production.
</p><p>This approach mirrors the <a href="/wiki/OECD" title="OECD">OECD</a> (Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development) definition given above.
</p>
<ol><li>Estimate the gross value of domestic output out of the many various economic activities;</li>
<li>Determine the <a href="/wiki/Intermediate_consumption" title="Intermediate consumption">intermediate consumption</a>, i.e., the cost of material, supplies and services used to produce final goods or services.</li>
<li>Deduct intermediate consumption from gross value to obtain the gross value added.</li></ol>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gross_value_added" title="Gross value added">Gross value added</a> = gross value of output – value of intermediate consumption.
</p><p>Value of output = value of the total sales of goods and services plus the value of changes in the inventory.
</p><p>The sum of the gross value added in the various economic activities is known as "GDP at factor cost".
</p><p>GDP at factor cost plus indirect taxes less subsidies on products = "GDP at producer price".
</p><p>For measuring the output of domestic product, economic activities (i.e. industries) are classified into various sectors. After classifying economic activities, the output of each sector is calculated by any of the following two methods:
</p>
<ol><li>By multiplying the output of each sector by their respective market price and adding them together</li>
<li>By collecting data on gross sales and inventories from the records of companies and adding them together</li></ol>
<p>The value of output of all sectors is then added to get the gross value of output at factor cost. Subtracting each sector's intermediate consumption from gross output value gives the GVA (=GDP) at factor cost. Adding indirect tax minus subsidies to GVA (GDP) at factor cost gives the "GVA (GDP) at producer prices".
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Income_approach">Income approach</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Income approach"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right skin-invert-image" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._GDP_Income_Basis.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/U.S._GDP_Income_Basis.png/500px-U.S._GDP_Income_Basis.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="178" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/U.S._GDP_Income_Basis.png/960px-U.S._GDP_Income_Basis.png 1.5x" data-file-width="1246" data-file-height="634" /></a><figcaption>U.S 2015 GDP computed on the income basis</figcaption></figure>
<p>The second way of estimating GDP is to use "the sum of primary incomes distributed by resident producer units".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>If GDP is calculated this way it is sometimes called gross domestic income (GDI), or GDP (I). GDI should provide the same amount as the expenditure method described later. By definition, GDI is equal to GDP. In practice, however, measurement errors will make the two figures slightly off when reported by national statistical agencies.
</p><p>This method measures GDP by adding incomes that firms pay households for factors of production they hire – wages for labour, interest for capital, rent for land and profits for entrepreneurship.
</p><p>The US "National Income and Product Accounts" divide incomes into five categories:
</p>
<ol><li>Wages, salaries, and supplementary labor income</li>
<li>Corporate profits</li>
<li>Interest and miscellaneous investment income</li>
<li>Income earned by sole proprietors and from the Housing subsector (net of expenses)</li>
<li>Net income from transfer payments from businesses</li></ol>
<p>These five income components sum to net domestic income at factor cost.
</p><p>Two adjustments must be made to get GDP:
</p>
<ol><li>Taxes on production and imports minus subsidies are added to get from factor cost to market prices.</li>
<li>Depreciation (or <a href="/wiki/Capital_consumption_allowance" class="mw-redirect" title="Capital consumption allowance">capital consumption allowance</a>) is added to get from net domestic product to gross domestic product.</li></ol>
<p>Total income can be subdivided according to various schemes, leading to various formulae for GDP measured by the income approach. A common one is:<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="Common where? (February 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><b>GDP</b> = <span class="sfrac nowrap;"><span style="display:none; display:inline-block; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:0.8em; font-size:70%;">Compensation of employees</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em;"><b>COE</b></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap;"><span style="display:none; display:inline-block; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:0.8em; font-size:70%;"><a href="/wiki/Gross_operating_surplus" title="Gross operating surplus">gross operating surplus</a></span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em;"><b>GOS</b></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap;"><span style="display:none; display:inline-block; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:0.8em; font-size:70%;"><a href="/wiki/Gross_mixed_income" class="mw-redirect" title="Gross mixed income">gross mixed income</a></span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em;"><b>GMI</b></span></span></span> + <span class="sfrac nowrap;"><span style="display:none; display:inline-block; text-align:center;"><span style="display:block; line-height:0.8em; font-size:70%;"> taxes less subsidies on production and imports</span><span style="display:block; line-height:1em;"><b>T<sub>P&amp;M</sub></b> − <b>S<sub>P&amp;M</sub></b></span></span></span></dd></dl>
<ul><li><b>Compensation of employees</b> (<b>COE</b>) measures the total remuneration to employees for work done. It includes wages and salaries, as well as employer contributions to <a href="/wiki/Social_security" class="mw-redirect" title="Social security">social security</a> and other such programs.</li>
<li><b>Gross operating surplus</b> (<b>GOS</b>) is the surplus due to owners of incorporated businesses. Often called <a href="/wiki/Profit_(accounting)" title="Profit (accounting)">profits</a>, although only a subset of total costs are subtracted from <a href="/wiki/Gross_output" title="Gross output">gross output</a> to calculate GOS.</li>
<li><b>Gross mixed income</b> (<b>GMI</b>) is the same measure as GOS, but for unincorporated businesses. This often includes most small businesses.</li></ul>
<p>The sum of <b>COE</b>, <b>GOS</b> and <b>GMI</b> is called total factor income; it is the income of all of the factors of production in society. It measures the value of GDP at factor (basic) prices. The difference between basic prices and final prices (those used in the expenditure calculation) is the total taxes and subsidies that the government has levied or paid on that production. So adding taxes less subsidies on production and imports converts GDP(I) at <a href="/wiki/Factor_cost" title="Factor cost">factor cost</a> to GDP(I) at final prices.
</p><p>Total factor income is also sometimes expressed as:
</p>
<dl><dd><i>Total factor income</i> = <i>employee compensation</i> + <i>corporate profits</i> + <i>proprietor's income</i> + <i>rental income</i> + <i>net interest</i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></dd></dl>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Expenditure_approach">Expenditure approach</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Expenditure approach"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The third way to estimate GDP is to calculate the sum of the final uses of goods and services (all uses except intermediate consumption) measured in purchasers' prices.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Market goods that are produced are purchased by someone. In the case where a good is produced and unsold, the standard accounting convention is that the producer has bought the good from themselves. Therefore, measuring the total expenditure used to buy things is a way of measuring production. This is known as the expenditure method of calculating GDP.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Components_of_GDP_by_expenditure">Components of GDP by expenditure</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Components of GDP by expenditure"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size skin-invert-image" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:GDP_Categories_-_United_States.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/GDP_Categories_-_United_States.png/500px-GDP_Categories_-_United_States.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="263" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/GDP_Categories_-_United_States.png/960px-GDP_Categories_-_United_States.png 1.5x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a><figcaption>U.S. GDP computed on the expenditure basis</figcaption></figure>
<p><b>GDP (Y)</b> is the sum of <b>consumption (C)</b>, <b>investment (I)</b>, <b>government expenditures (G)</b> and <b>net exports (X − M)</b>.
</p>
<dl><dd><b>Y</b> = <b>C</b> + <b>I</b> + <b>G</b> + <b>(X − M)</b></dd></dl>
<p>Here is a description of each GDP component:
</p>
<ul><li><b>C (consumption)</b> is normally the largest GDP component in the economy, consisting of private expenditures in the economy (<a href="/wiki/Household_final_consumption_expenditure" title="Household final consumption expenditure">household final consumption expenditure</a>). These personal expenditures fall under one of the following categories: <a href="/wiki/Durable_good" title="Durable good">durable goods</a>, nondurable goods, and services. Examples include food, rent, jewelry, gasoline, and medical expenses, but not the purchase of new housing.</li>
<li><b>I (investment)</b> includes, for instance, business investment in equipment, but does not include exchanges of existing assets. Examples include the construction of a new <a href="/wiki/Mining" title="Mining">mine</a>, the purchase of software, or the purchase of machinery and equipment for a factory. Spending by households (not the government) on new houses is also included in investment. In contrast to its colloquial meaning, "investment" in GDP does not mean purchases related to financial investments. Buying <a href="/wiki/Financial_services" title="Financial services">financial products</a> is classed as '<a href="/wiki/Saving" title="Saving">saving</a>', as opposed to investment. This avoids double-counting: if one buys shares in a company, and the company uses the money received to build a plant, purchase equipment, etc., the amount will be counted toward GDP when the company spends the money on those things; to also count it when one gives it to the company would be to count two times an amount that only corresponds to one group of products. Buying <a href="/wiki/Bond_(finance)" title="Bond (finance)">bonds</a> or companies' <a href="/wiki/Share_capital" title="Share capital">equity shares</a> is a swapping of <a href="/wiki/Deed" title="Deed">deeds</a>, a transfer of claims on future production, not directly an expenditure on products; buying an existing building will involve a positive investment by the buyer and a negative investment by the seller, netting to zero overall investment.</li>
<li><b>G (government spending)</b> is the sum of <a href="/wiki/Government_spending" title="Government spending">government expenditures</a> on <a href="/wiki/Final_good" title="Final good">final goods</a> and services. It includes salaries of <a href="/wiki/Public_servants" class="mw-redirect" title="Public servants">public servants</a>, purchases of weapons for the military and any investment expenditure by a government. It does not include any <a href="/wiki/Transfer_payment" title="Transfer payment">transfer payments</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Social_security" class="mw-redirect" title="Social security">social security</a> or <a href="/wiki/Unemployment_benefits" title="Unemployment benefits">unemployment benefits</a>. Analyses outside the US will often treat government investment as part of <i>investment</i> rather than <i>government spending</i>.</li>
<li><b>X (exports)</b> represents gross exports. GDP captures the amount a country produces, including goods and services produced for other nations' consumption, therefore exports are added.</li>
<li><b>M (imports)</b> represents gross imports. Imports are subtracted since imported goods will be included in the terms <b>C</b>, <b>I</b>, and <b>G</b>, and must be deducted to avoid counting foreign <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">supply</a> as domestic.</li></ul>
<p><b>C</b>, <b>I</b>, and <b>G</b> are expenditures on <a href="/wiki/Final_good" title="Final good">final goods</a> and services; expenditures on intermediate goods and services do not count. (Intermediate goods and services are those used by businesses to produce other goods and services within the accounting year.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>) So for example if a <a href="/wiki/Car_manufacturer" class="mw-redirect" title="Car manufacturer">car manufacturer</a> buys <a href="/wiki/Auto_parts" class="mw-redirect" title="Auto parts">auto parts</a>, assembles the car and sells it, only the final car sold is counted towards the GDP. Meanwhile, if a person buys replacement auto parts to install them on their car, those are counted towards the GDP.
</p><p>According to the U.S. Bureau of Economic Analysis, which is responsible for calculating the national accounts in the United States, "In general, the source data for the expenditures components are considered more reliable than those for the income components [see income method, above]."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Encyclopedia Britannica records an alternate way of measuring exports minus imports: notating it as the single variable NX.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Nominal_GDP_and_real_GDP">Nominal GDP and real GDP</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Nominal GDP and real GDP"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The raw GDP figure given by the equations above is called the nominal, historical, or current GDP. When comparing GDP figures from one year to another, compensating for changes in the value of money—for the effects of inflation or deflation—is desirable. To make it more meaningful for year-to-year comparisons, a nominal GDP may be multiplied by the ratio between the value of money in the year the GDP was measured and the value of money in a base year.
</p><p>For example, suppose a country's GDP in 1990 was <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7008100000000000000♠"></span>$100&#160;million</span> and its GDP in 2000 was <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7008300000000000000♠"></span>$300&#160;million</span>. Suppose also that inflation had halved the value of its currency over that period. To meaningfully compare its GDP in 2000 to its GDP in 1990, we could multiply the GDP in 2000 by one-half, to make it relative to 1990 as a base year. The result would be that the GDP in 2000 equals <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7008300000000000000♠"></span>$300&#160;million</span> × <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1154941027">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);clip-path:polygon(0px 0px,0px 0px,0px 0px);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style><span class="frac"><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">2</span></span> = <span class="nowrap"><span data-sort-value="7008150000000000000♠"></span>$150&#160;million</span>, <i>in 1990 monetary terms.</i> We would see that the country's GDP had realistically increased 50 <a href="/wiki/Percentage#Percentage_increase_and_decrease" title="Percentage">percent</a> over that period, not 200 percent, as it might appear from the raw GDP data. The GDP adjusted for changes in money value in this way is called the <a href="/wiki/Real_gross_domestic_product" title="Real gross domestic product">real GDP</a>.
</p><p>The factor used to convert GDP from current to constant values in this way is called the <i><a href="/wiki/GDP_deflator" title="GDP deflator">GDP deflator</a></i>. Unlike <a href="/wiki/Consumer_price_index" title="Consumer price index">consumer price index</a>, which measures inflation or deflation in the price of household consumer goods, the GDP deflator measures changes in the prices of all domestically produced goods and services in an economy including investment goods and government services, as well as household consumption goods.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="National_measurement">National measurement</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: National measurement"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/National_agencies_responsible_for_GDP_measurement" title="National agencies responsible for GDP measurement">National agencies responsible for GDP measurement</a></div>
<p>Within each country GDP is normally measured by a national government statistical agency, as <a href="/wiki/Private_sector" title="Private sector">private sector</a> organizations normally do not have access to the information required (especially information on expenditure and production by governments).
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="International_standards">International standards</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: International standards"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The international standard for measuring GDP is contained in the book <i><a href="/wiki/United_Nations_System_of_National_Accounts_(UNSNA)" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations System of National Accounts (UNSNA)">System of National Accounts</a></i> (2008), which was prepared by representatives of the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Organisation_for_Economic_Co-operation_and_Development" class="mw-redirect" title="Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development">Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" class="mw-redirect" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>. The publication is normally referred to as SNA2008 to distinguish it from the previous edition published in 1993 (SNA93) or 1968 (called SNA68)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Central&#95;Bureau&#95;of&#95;Statistics&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Central_Bureau_of_Statistics-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>SNA2008 provides a set of rules and procedures for the measurement of national accounts. The standards are designed to be flexible, to allow for differences in local statistical needs and conditions.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Problems_with_GDP_data">Problems with GDP data</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Problems with GDP data"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Economic_data" title="Economic data">Economic data</a></div>
<p>A peer-reviewed study published in the <i><a href="/wiki/Journal_of_Political_Economy" title="Journal of Political Economy">Journal of Political Economy</a></i> in October 2022 found signs of manipulation of <a href="/wiki/Economic_growth" title="Economic growth">economic growth</a> statistics in the majority of countries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:6&#95;25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This manipulation was greatest in countries that were semi-authoritarian/authoritarian, or did not have effective <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_powers" title="Separation of powers">separation of powers</a>. The study took the annual growth in the brightness of lights at night, as measured by satellites, and compared it to officially reported economic growth. Authoritarian states consistently reported higher growth in GDP than the growth in night lights would suggest, an effect that could not be explained by economic structures, sector composition or other factors.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:6&#95;25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Corporate_haven" title="Corporate haven">Corporate havens</a> can also have a <a href="/wiki/Corporate_haven#Distorted_GDP/GNP" title="Corporate haven">distorted GDP</a>.
</p>

</div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Economic_growth">Economic growth</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Economic growth"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Economic_growth" title="Economic growth">Economic growth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Productivity-improving_technologies" title="Productivity-improving technologies">Productivity-improving technologies</a></div>
