</td></tr></tbody></table></div></figcaption></figure>
<p>Real GDP can be used to calculate the GDP growth rate, which indicates how much a country's production has increased (or decreased, if the growth rate is negative) compared to the previous year, typically expressed as <a href="/wiki/Relative_change#Percentage_change" title="Relative change">percentage change</a>. The economic growth can be expressed as:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_real_GDP_growth_rate" title="List of countries by real GDP growth rate">List of countries by real GDP growth rate</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_past_and_projected_GDP_(nominal)" title="List of countries by past and projected GDP (nominal)">List of countries by past and projected GDP (nominal)</a> (<a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_past_and_projected_GDP_(PPP)" title="List of countries by past and projected GDP (PPP)">PPP</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_real_GDP_per_capita_growth" title="List of countries by real GDP per capita growth">List of countries by real GDP per capita growth</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GNI_per_capita_growth" title="List of countries by GNI per capita growth">List of countries by GNI per capita growth</a></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Relation_to_gross_national_income">Relation to gross national income</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Relation to gross national income"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>GDP can be contrasted with the <a href="/wiki/Gross_national_income" title="Gross national income">gross national income</a> (GNI) also known as gross national product (GNP). The difference is that GDP defines its scope according to location, while GNI defines its scope according to ownership. In a global context, <a href="/wiki/Gross_world_product" title="Gross world product">world GDP and world GNI</a> are, therefore, equivalent terms.
</p><p>GDP is a product produced within a country's borders; GNI is product produced by enterprises owned by a country's citizens. The two would be the same if all of the productive enterprises in a country were owned by its own citizens and those citizens did not own productive enterprises in any other countries. In practice, however, foreign ownership makes GDP and GNI non-identical. Production within a country's borders, but by an enterprise owned by somebody outside the country, counts as part of its GDP but not its GNI; on the other hand, production by an enterprise located outside the country, but owned by one of its citizens, counts as part of its GNI but not its GDP.
For example, the GNI of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">US</a> is the value of output produced by American-owned firms, regardless of where the firms are located. 
</p><p>Gross national income (GNI) equals GDP plus income receipts from the rest of the world minus income payments to the rest of the world.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 1991, the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> switched from using GNP to using GDP as its primary measure of production.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The relationship between United States GDP and GNP is shown in table 1.7.5 of the <i><a href="/wiki/National_Income_and_Product_Accounts" title="National Income and Product Accounts">National Income and Product Accounts</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Other examples that amplify differences between GDP and GNI can be found by comparing indicators of developed and developing countries. The GDP of Japan for 2020 was 5.05559 trillion.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Predictably, as a developed country, Japan has a higher GNI of 5.16915 trillion for the same year,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> an increase of 113.560 million. This is indicative of the production level in the country being higher than that of national production. On the other hand, the case with <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> is the opposite with its GNI in 2023 being lower than its GDP by 3.85 billion. This shows countries that receive investments and foreign aid from abroad.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Limitations_and_criticisms">Limitations and criticisms</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Limitations and criticisms"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Simon_Kuznets" title="Simon Kuznets">Simon Kuznets</a>, the economist who developed the first comprehensive set of measures of national income, stated in his second report to the U.S. Congress in 1937, in a section titled "Uses and Abuses of National Income Measurements":<sup id="cite&#95;ref-kuznets&#95;13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kuznets-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p> The valuable capacity of the human mind to simplify a complex situation in a compact characterization becomes dangerous when not controlled in terms of definitely stated criteria. With quantitative measurements especially, the definiteness of the result suggests, often misleadingly, a precision and simplicity in the outlines of the object measured. Measurements of national income are subject to this type of illusion and resulting abuse, especially since they deal with matters that are the center of conflict of opposing social groups where the effectiveness of an argument is often contingent upon oversimplification. [...]
All these qualifications upon estimates of national income as an index of productivity are just as important when income measurements are interpreted from the point of view of economic welfare. But in the latter case additional difficulties will be suggested to anyone who wants to penetrate below the surface of total figures and market values. Economic welfare cannot be adequately measured unless the personal distribution of income is known. And no income measurement undertakes to estimate the reverse side of income, that is, the intensity and unpleasantness of effort going into the earning of income. The welfare of a nation can, therefore, scarcely be inferred from a measurement of national income as defined above.</p></blockquote><p> In 1962, Kuznets stated:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p><blockquote><p>Distinctions must be kept in mind between quantity and quality of growth, between costs and returns, and between the short and long run. Goals for more growth should specify more growth of what and for what.</p></blockquote>
<p>GDP as initially defined includes spending on goods and services that would shrink if underlying problems were solved or reduced - for example, medical care, crime-fighting, and the military. During World War II, Kuznets came to argue that military spending should be excluded during peacetime. This idea did not become popular; these activities are tracked because they fit into macroeconomic models (e.g. military spending uses up capital and labor).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Ever since the development of GDP, multiple observers have pointed out limitations of using GDP as the overarching measure of economic and social progress. Furthermore, the GDP does not consider human health nor the educational aspect of a population.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Instances of GDP measures have been considered numbers that are artificial constructs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> American politician <a href="/wiki/Robert_F._Kennedy" title="Robert F. Kennedy">Robert F. Kennedy</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> criticized GDP (or GNI), listing many examples of bad things it does count and good things it does not count:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Gross National Product counts air pollution and cigarette advertising, and ambulances to clear our highways of carnage.  It counts special locks for our doors and the jails for the people who break them.  It counts the destruction of the redwood and the loss of our natural wonder in chaotic sprawl.  It counts napalm and counts nuclear warheads and armored cars for the police to fight the riots in our cities.  It counts Whitman's rifle and Speck's knife, and the television programs which glorify violence in order to sell toys to our children.  Yet the gross national product does not allow for the health of our children, the quality of their education or the joy of their play.  It does not include the beauty of our poetry or the strength of our marriages, the intelligence of our public debate or the integrity of our public officials.  It measures neither our wit nor our courage, neither our wisdom nor our learning, neither our compassion nor our devotion to our country, it measures everything in short, except that which makes life worthwhile.  And it can tell us everything about America except why we are proud that we are Americans.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Deficit_spending">Deficit spending</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Deficit spending"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Deficit_spending" title="Deficit spending">Deficit spending</a> increases GDP in case of positive <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_multiplier" title="Fiscal multiplier">fiscal multipliers</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> GDP as a metric can incentivize politicians to <a href="/wiki/Overspending" title="Overspending">overspend</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Counterfactual GDP under a <a href="/wiki/Balanced_budget" title="Balanced budget">balanced budget</a> scenario can be estimated.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>If a country becomes increasingly in debt, and spends large amounts of income on debt <a href="/wiki/Interest_expense" title="Interest expense">interest expense</a>, will be reflected in a decreased GNI<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> but not a decreased GDP. Similarly, if a country sells off its resources to entities outside their country this will also be reflected over time in decreased GNI, but not decreased GDP. This would make the use of GDP more attractive for politicians in countries with increasing national debt and decreasing assets.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Unpaid_work">Unpaid work</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Unpaid work"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>GDP excludes the value of household and other <a href="/wiki/Unpaid_work" title="Unpaid work">unpaid work</a>. Some, including <a href="/wiki/Martha_Nussbaum" title="Martha Nussbaum">Martha Nussbaum</a>, argue that this value should be included in measuring GDP, as household labor is largely a <a href="/wiki/Substitute_good" title="Substitute good">substitute</a> for goods and services that would otherwise be purchased with money.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Even under conservative estimates, the value of unpaid labor in Australia has been calculated to be over 50% of the country's GDP.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A later study analyzed this value in other countries, with results ranging from a low of about 15% in Canada (using conservative estimates) to high of nearly 70% in the United Kingdom (using more liberal estimates). For the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, the value was estimated to be between about 20% on the low end to nearly 50% on the high end, depending on the methodology being used.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Because many public policies are shaped by GDP calculations and by the related field of <a href="/wiki/National_accounts" title="National accounts">national accounts</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> public policy might differ if unpaid work were included in total GDP. Some economists have advocated for changes in the way public policies are formed and implemented.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some have pointed out that GDP did not adapt to sociotechnical changes to give a more accurate picture of the modern economy and does not encapsulate the <a href="/wiki/Value_(economics)" title="Value (economics)">value</a> of new activities such as delivering price-free information and entertainment on <a href="/wiki/Social_media" title="Social media">social media</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:5&#95;50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2017 <a href="/wiki/Diane_Coyle" title="Diane Coyle">Diane Coyle</a> explained that GDP excludes much unpaid work, writing that "many people contribute free digital <a href="/wiki/Work_(human_activity)" title="Work (human activity)">work</a> such as writing <a href="/wiki/FOSS" class="mw-redirect" title="FOSS">open-source software</a> that can substitute for marketed equivalents, and it clearly has great economic value despite a price of zero", which constitutes a common criticism "of the reliance on GDP as the measure of economic success" especially after the emergence of the <a href="/wiki/Digital_economy" title="Digital economy">digital economy</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> A 2025 study in the <i>American Economic Journal</i> devised a new GDP measurement (GDP-B) that accounts for the welfare value of new goods and free goods.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:5&#95;50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2019, Erik Brynjolfsson and Avinash Collis argued that GDP does not reflect the growing value of many digital goods because they have zero price.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Along with several coauthors, they proposed an alternative approach, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://digitaleconomy.stanford.edu/research/gdp-b-a-new-well-being-metric-in-the-era-of-the-digital-economy/">GDP-B</a>, which is based on measuring the <i>benefits</i> of goods and services, rather than their price or cost.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Health">Health</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Health"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In 2013 scientists reported that large <a href="/wiki/Health_care" title="Health care">improvements</a> in <a href="/wiki/Health" title="Health">health</a> only lead to modest long-term increases in GDP per capita.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> After developing an abstract metric similar to GDP, the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Partnership_Studies" class="mw-redirect" title="Center for Partnership Studies">Center for Partnership Studies</a> highlighted that GDP "and other metrics that reflect and perpetuate them" may not be useful for facilitating the production of products and provision of services that are useful – or comparatively more useful – to society, and instead may "actually encourage, rather than discourage, destructive activities".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The number of <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obese</a> adults was approximately 600&#160;million (12%) in 2015.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NEJM2017&#95;57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NEJM2017-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Environment">Environment</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Environment"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Eco-economic_decoupling" title="Eco-economic decoupling">Eco-economic decoupling</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size skin-invert-image" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Absolute-decoupling-Growth-and-falling-emissions-all.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Absolute-decoupling-Growth-and-falling-emissions-all.png/500px-Absolute-decoupling-Growth-and-falling-emissions-all.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="413" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Absolute-decoupling-Growth-and-falling-emissions-all.png/960px-Absolute-decoupling-Growth-and-falling-emissions-all.png 1.5x" data-file-width="2175" data-file-height="2565" /></a><figcaption>Eco-economic decoupling between GDP growth and <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas emissions">greenhouse gas emissions</a> decrease</figcaption></figure>
<p>Many <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">environmentalists</a> argue that GDP is a poor measure of social progress because it does not take into account harm to the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">environment</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the language of economics, everything comes down to its monetary value.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In essence, GDP rewards behaviors that are detrimental to the environment.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> GDP also does not capture certain phenomena impacting citizens' well-being.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For example, traffic jams could cause GDP to increase as there is a higher consumption of gasoline, however, GDP fails to consider citizens' well-being in terms of the quality of air due to air pollution from the traffic jams.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Various alternatives have been developed(see below).
</p><p>A 2020 study found that "poor regions' GDP grows faster by attracting more polluting <a href="/wiki/Production_(economics)" title="Production (economics)">production</a> after connection to China's expressway system.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> GDP may not be a tool capable of recognizing how much <a href="/wiki/Natural_capital" title="Natural capital">natural capital</a> agents of the economy are building or protecting.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
In 2020 scientists, as part of a <a href="/wiki/World_Scientists%27_Warning_to_Humanity" title="World Scientists&#39; Warning to Humanity">World Scientists' Warning to Humanity</a>-associated series, warned that worldwide growth in <a href="/wiki/Affluence" class="mw-redirect" title="Affluence">affluence</a> in terms of GDP-metrics has increased <a href="/wiki/Resource_use" class="mw-redirect" title="Resource use">resource use</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pollutant" title="Pollutant">pollutant emissions</a> with affluent <a href="/wiki/Global_citizen" class="mw-redirect" title="Global citizen">citizens of the world</a> – in terms of e.g. resource-intensive consumption – being responsible for most negative environmental impacts and central to a transition to safer, <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainable</a> conditions. They summarised evidence, presented solution approaches and stated that far-reaching <a href="/wiki/Lifestyle_(sociology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lifestyle (sociology)">lifestyle</a> <a href="/wiki/Behavioural_change_theories" title="Behavioural change theories">changes</a> need to <a href="/wiki/Technology_and_society" title="Technology and society">complement technological advancements</a> and that existing societies, economies and cultures <a href="/wiki/Incentive" title="Incentive">incite</a> <a href="/wiki/Overconsumption" title="Overconsumption">consumption expansion</a> and that the <a href="/wiki/Structural_change" title="Structural change">structural imperative</a> for <a href="/wiki/Economic_growth" title="Economic growth">growth</a> in <a href="/wiki/Competition_(economics)" title="Competition (economics)">competitive</a> <a href="/wiki/Market_economy" title="Market economy">market economies</a> inhibits <a href="/wiki/Societal_change" class="mw-redirect" title="Societal change">societal change</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-phys-affluence&#95;65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-phys-affluence-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-gdp-affluence&#95;67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gdp-affluence-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Sarah Arnold, Senior Economist at the <a href="/wiki/New_Economics_Foundation" title="New Economics Foundation">New Economics Foundation</a> (NEF) stated that "GDP includes activities that are detrimental to our <a href="/wiki/Economy" title="Economy">economy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">society</a> in the long term, such as <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, strip mining, overfishing and so on".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The number of trees that are net lost annually is estimated to be approximately 10&#160;billion.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The global average annual deforested land in the 2015–2020 demi-decade was 10&#160;million hectares and the average annual net forest area loss in the 2000–2010 decade 4.7&#160;million hectares, according to the <a href="/wiki/Global_Forest_Resources_Assessment_(FRA)" class="mw-redirect" title="Global Forest Resources Assessment (FRA)">Global Forest Resources Assessment 2020</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to one study, depending on the level of <a href="/wiki/Wealth_inequality" class="mw-redirect" title="Wealth inequality">wealth inequality</a>, higher GDP-growth can be associated with more deforestation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2019 "agriculture and <a href="/wiki/Agribusiness" title="Agribusiness">agribusiness</a>" accounted for 24% of the GDP of Brazil, <a href="/wiki/2019_Amazon_rainforest_wildfires" title="2019 Amazon rainforest wildfires">where a large share of annual net tropical forest loss occurred and is associated with sizable portions of this economic activity domain</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Steven_A._Cohen_(academic)" title="Steven A. Cohen (academic)">Steve Cohen</a> of the Earth Institute elucidated that while GDP does not distinguish between different activities (or <a href="/wiki/Lifestyle_(sociology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Lifestyle (sociology)">lifestyles</a>), "all consumption behaviors are not created equal and do not have the same impact on <a href="/wiki/Environmental_sustainability" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental sustainability">environmental sustainability</a>".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Johan_Rockstr%C3%B6m" title="Johan Rockström">Johan Rockström</a>, director of the Potsdam Institute for Climate Impact Research, noted that "it's difficult to see if the current G.D.P.-based <a href="/wiki/Economic_model" title="Economic model">model</a> of economic growth can go hand-in-hand with rapid <a href="/wiki/Mitigation_of_climate_change" class="mw-redirect" title="Mitigation of climate change">cutting of emissions</a>", which nations have agreed to attempt under the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Agreement" title="Paris Agreement">Paris Agreement</a> in order to mitigate real-world impacts of climate change.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 1989, <a href="/wiki/John_B._Cobb" title="John B. Cobb">John B. Cobb</a> and <a href="/wiki/Herman_Daly" title="Herman Daly">Herman Daly</a> introduced <a href="/wiki/Index_of_Sustainable_Economic_Welfare" title="Index of Sustainable Economic Welfare">Index of Sustainable Economic Welfare</a> (ISEW) by taking into account other factors such as consumption of nonrenewable resources and degradation of the environment. ISEW is roughly defined as: personal consumption + public non-defensive expenditures − private defensive expenditures + capital formation + services from domestic labour − costs of <a href="/wiki/Environmental_degradation" title="Environmental degradation">environmental degradation</a> − depreciation of natural capital.
In 2005, <a href="/wiki/Med_Jones" title="Med Jones">Med Jones</a>, an American Economist, at the International Institute of Management, introduced the first secular Gross National Happiness Index a.k.a. <a href="/wiki/Gross_National_Well-being" title="Gross National Well-being">Gross National Well-being</a> framework and Index to complement GDP economics with additional seven dimensions, including environment, education, and government, work, social and health (mental and physical) indicators. The proposal was inspired by the King of Bhutan's <a href="/wiki/Gross_National_Happiness" title="Gross National Happiness">GNH</a> philosophy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
In 2019, Serge Pierre Besanger published a "GDP 3.0" proposal which combines an expanded GNI formula which he calls GNIX, with a <a href="/wiki/Palma_ratio" class="mw-redirect" title="Palma ratio">Palma ratio</a> and a set of environmental metrics based on the Daly Rule.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The UK's <a href="/wiki/Natural_Capital_Committee" title="Natural Capital Committee">Natural Capital Committee</a> highlighted the shortcomings of GDP in its advice to the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">UK</a> Government in 2013, pointing out that GDP "focuses on flows, not stocks. As a result, an economy can run down its assets yet, at the same time, record high levels of GDP growth, until a point is reached where the depleted assets act as a check on future growth". They then went on to say that "it is apparent that the recorded GDP growth rate overstates the <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_growth" class="mw-redirect" title="Sustainable growth">sustainable growth</a> rate. Broader measures of wellbeing and wealth are needed for this and there is a danger that short-term decisions based solely on what is currently measured by national accounts may prove to be costly in the long-term".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2025)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>China launched the <a href="/wiki/Gross_Ecosystem_Product_(GEP)" class="mw-redirect" title="Gross Ecosystem Product (GEP)">Gross Ecosystem Product (GEP)</a> in 2020. It measures the contribution of ecosystems to the economy, including by regulating climate. It spread widely across the country. The first province to issue local rules about GEP was <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang </a>, and a year later it has already decided the fate of a project in the <a href="/wiki/Deqing_County,_Zhejiang" title="Deqing County, Zhejiang">Deqing</a> region. For example, the GEP of Chengtian Radon Spring Nature Reserve has been calculated as US$43 million.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="GDP_per_capita">GDP per capita</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: GDP per capita"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita_in_2024.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Map_of_countries_by_GDP_%28PPP%29_per_capita_in_2024.svg/500px-Map_of_countries_by_GDP_%28PPP%29_per_capita_in_2024.svg.png" decoding="async" width="350" height="181" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Map_of_countries_by_GDP_%28PPP%29_per_capita_in_2024.svg/960px-Map_of_countries_by_GDP_%28PPP%29_per_capita_in_2024.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="5834" data-file-height="3021" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_(PPP)_per_capita" title="List of countries by GDP (PPP) per capita">Countries by GDP (PPP) per capita</a> in 2024 <table width="100%h">  <tbody><tr> <td valign="top"> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" /><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#00008a; color:white;-webkit-print-color-adjust: exact; print-color-adjust: exact;">&#160;</span>&#160;&gt;&#160;$60,000</div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" /><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#003c00; color:white;-webkit-print-color-adjust: exact; print-color-adjust: exact;">&#160;</span>&#160;$50,000–$60,000</div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" /><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#008f00; color:black;-webkit-print-color-adjust: exact; print-color-adjust: exact;">&#160;</span>&#160;$40,000–$50,000</div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" /><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#00f900; color:black;-webkit-print-color-adjust: exact; print-color-adjust: exact;">&#160;</span>&#160;$30,000–$40,000</div> </td> <td valign="top"> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" /><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#b3ff00; color:black;-webkit-print-color-adjust: exact; print-color-adjust: exact;">&#160;</span>&#160;$20,000–$30,000</div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" /><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffff00; color:black;-webkit-print-color-adjust: exact; print-color-adjust: exact;">&#160;</span>&#160;$10,000–$20,000</div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" /><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffd215; color:black;-webkit-print-color-adjust: exact; print-color-adjust: exact;">&#160;</span>&#160;$5,000–$10,000</div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" /><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff852f; color:black;-webkit-print-color-adjust: exact; print-color-adjust: exact;">&#160;</span>&#160;$2,500–$5,000</div> </td> <td valign="top"> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" /><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff0000; color:black;-webkit-print-color-adjust: exact; print-color-adjust: exact;">&#160;</span>&#160;$1,000–$2,500</div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" /><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#a30000; color:white;-webkit-print-color-adjust: exact; print-color-adjust: exact;">&#160;</span>&#160;&lt;&#160;$1,000</div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" /><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#b9b9b9; color:black;-webkit-print-color-adjust: exact; print-color-adjust: exact;">&#160;</span>&#160;No data</div> </td></tr></tbody></table> </figcaption></figure>
<p>GDP changes with <a href="/wiki/Population_change" title="Population change">population change</a>. GDP adjusted for population is called <i>Per-capita GDP</i> or <i>GDP per person</i>. This measures the average production of a person in the country. The major advantage of GDP per capita as an indicator of the standard of living is that it is measured frequently, widely, and consistently. It is measured frequently in that most countries provide information on GDP every quarter, allowing trends to be seen quickly. It is measured widely in that some measure of GDP is available for almost every country in the world, allowing inter-country comparisons. It is measured consistently in that the technical definition of GDP is relatively consistent among countries.
</p><p>It can be argued that GDP per capita is an indicator of standard of living.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-HDWMSL&#95;81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HDWMSL-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As a result, GDP per capita as a standard of living is a continued usage because most people have a fairly accurate idea of what it is and know it is tough to come up with quantitative measures for such constructs as happiness, quality of life, and well-being.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-HDWMSL&#95;81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HDWMSL-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> From the perspective of environmental, social and governance (ESG) measures, GDP per capita trends can be influenced by factors such as gender parity and elements of regulatory quality. The change in number of MSMEs (Micro, Small, and Medium Enterprises) in the Philippines from 2008 through 2021 would be an example of elements such as the per capita gross domestic product and unemployment rate having significant effect on a developing country with mixed economy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Lists_of_countries_by_GDP_per_capita">Lists of countries by GDP per capita</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Lists of countries by GDP per capita"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>

</div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Standard_of_living">Standard of living</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gross_domestic_product&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Standard of living"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Although a high or rising level of GDP per capita is often associated with increased economic and social progress, the opposite sometimes occurs. For example, <a href="/wiki/Jean_Dr%C3%A8ze" title="Jean Drèze">Jean Drèze</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amartya_Sen" title="Amartya Sen">Amartya Sen</a> have pointed out that an increase in GDP or in GDP growth does not necessarily lead to a higher standard of living, particularly in areas such as healthcare and education.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another important area that does not necessarily improve along with GDP is political liberty, which is most notable in China, where GDP growth is strong yet political liberties are heavily restricted.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> GDP does not account for the distribution of income among the residents of a country, because GDP is merely an aggregate measure. An economy may be highly developed or growing rapidly, but also contain a wide gap between the rich and the poor in a society. These inequalities often occur on the lines of race, ethnicity, gender, religion, or other minority status within countries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This can lead to misleading characterizations of economic well-being if the <a href="/wiki/Income_distribution" title="Income distribution">income distribution</a> is heavily skewed toward the high end, as the poorer residents will not directly benefit from the overall level of wealth and income generated in their country (their purchasing power can decline, even as the mean <a href="/wiki/GDP_per_capita" class="mw-redirect" title="GDP per capita">GDP per capita</a> rises). GDP per capita measures (like aggregate GDP measures) do not account for income distribution (and tend to overstate the average income per capita). For example, South Africa during apartheid ranked high in terms of GDP per capita, but the benefits of this immense wealth and income were not shared equally among its citizens.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The United Nations has aimed in its <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goal_10" title="Sustainable Development Goal 10">Sustainable Development Goals</a>, amongst other global initiatives, to address wealth inequality.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>GDP does not include several factors that influence the standard of living. In particular, it fails to account for:
</p>
<ul><li><b><a href="/wiki/Externalities" class="mw-redirect" title="Externalities">Externalities</a></b> – Economic growth may entail an increase in negative externalities that are not directly measured in GDP.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-soL&#95;89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-soL-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-limitations&#95;90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-limitations-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Increased industrial output might grow GDP, but any pollution is not counted.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>Non-market transactions</b> – GDP excludes activities that are not provided through the market, such as household production, bartering of goods and services, and volunteer or unpaid services.</li>
<li><b>Non-monetary economy</b> – GDP omits economies where no money comes into play at all, resulting in inaccurate or abnormally low GDP figures. For example, in countries with major business transactions occurring informally, portions of local economy are not easily registered. <a href="/wiki/Barter_(economics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Barter (economics)">Bartering</a> may be more prominent than the use of money, even extending to services.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-limitations&#95;90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-limitations-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b>Quality improvements and inclusion of new products</b> – by not fully adjusting for quality improvements and new products, GDP understates true <a href="/wiki/Economic_growth" title="Economic growth">economic growth</a>. For instance, although computers today are less expensive and more powerful than computers from the past, GDP treats them as the same products by only accounting for their monetary value. The introduction of new products is also difficult to measure accurately and is not reflected in GDP although it may increase the standard of living. For example, even the richest person in 1900 could not purchase standard products, such as antibiotics and cell phones, that an average consumer can buy today, since such modern conveniences did not exist then.</li>
<li><b>Sustainability of growth</b> – GDP is a measurement of economic historic activity and is not necessarily a projection.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Income_distribution" title="Income distribution">Income distribution</a></b> – GDP does not account for variances in incomes of various demographic groups. See <a href="/wiki/Income_inequality_metrics" title="Income inequality metrics">income inequality metrics</a> for discussion of a variety of inequality-based economic measures.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-limitations&#95;90-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-limitations-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Broken_window_fallacy" class="mw-redirect" title="Broken window fallacy">Broken window fallacy</a></b> - The Broken window fallacy is an economic phenomenon stating that GDP will treat money spent on repairing damages even though there isn’t a net benefit to society.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In other words GDP doesn’t account for the fact that there was destruction in the first place. For example if there is a natural disaster and the government spends money on repairing housing, the cost of repairing housing will be included in the government spending portion on GDP even though people might have been equally well off if there wasn’t government spending and a natural disaster.</li></ul>
<p>In the 1980s, <a href="/wiki/Amartya_Sen" title="Amartya Sen">Amartya Sen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Martha_Nussbaum" title="Martha Nussbaum">Martha Nussbaum</a> developed the <a href="/wiki/Capability_approach" title="Capability approach">capability approach</a>, which focuses on the functional capabilities enjoyed by people within a country, rather than the aggregate GDP within a country. These capabilities consist of the functions that a person is able to achieve.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
In 1990, <a href="/wiki/Mahbub_ul_Haq" title="Mahbub ul Haq">Mahbub ul Haq</a>, a Pakistani economist at the United Nations, introduced the <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> (HDI). The HDI is a composite index of life expectancy at birth, adult literacy rate and standard of living measured as a logarithmic function of GDP, adjusted to purchasing power parity.
In 2009 Professors <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stiglitz" title="Joseph Stiglitz">Joseph Stiglitz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amartya_Sen" title="Amartya Sen">Amartya Sen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Paul_Fitoussi" title="Jean-Paul Fitoussi">Jean-Paul Fitoussi</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Commission_on_the_Measurement_of_Economic_Performance_and_Social_Progress" title="Commission on the Measurement of Economic Performance and Social Progress">Commission on the Measurement of Economic Performance and Social Progress</a> (CMEPSP), formed by French President, <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_Sarkozy" title="Nicolas Sarkozy">Nicolas Sarkozy</a> published a proposal to overcome the limitation of GDP economics to expand the focus to well-being economics with a well-being framework consisting of health, environment, work, physical safety, economic safety, and political freedom. This has been adopted in a number of countries as a <a href="/wiki/Wellbeing_economy" title="Wellbeing economy">wellbeing economy</a> policy.
In 2008, the <a href="/wiki/Centre_for_Bhutan_Studies_and_GNH_Research" title="Centre for Bhutan Studies and GNH Research">Centre for Bhutan Studies</a> began publishing the <a href="/wiki/Gross_National_Happiness#Bhutanese_GNH_index" title="Gross National Happiness">Bhutan Gross National Happiness (GNH) Index</a>, whose contributors to happiness include physical, mental, and spiritual health; time balance; social and community vitality; cultural vitality; education; living standards; good governance; and ecological vitality.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
In 2013, the <a href="/wiki/OECD_Better_Life_Index" title="OECD Better Life Index">OECD Better Life Index</a> was published by the OECD. The dimensions of the index included health, economic, workplace, income, jobs, housing, civic engagement, and <a href="/wiki/Life_satisfaction" title="Life satisfaction">life satisfaction</a>.
Since 2012, John Helliwell, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Layard,_Baron_Layard" title="Richard Layard, Baron Layard">Richard Layard</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Sachs" title="Jeffrey Sachs">Jeffrey Sachs</a> have edited an annual <a href="/wiki/World_Happiness_Report" title="World Happiness Report">World Happiness Report</a> which reports a national measure of subjective well-being, derived from a single survey question on satisfaction with life. GDP explains some of the cross-national variation in life satisfaction, but more of it is explained by other, social variables.
</p>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2021-02-15</span></span>. <q>To convert GDP into GNI, it is necessary to add the income received by resident units from abroad and deduct the income created by production in the country but transferred to units residing abroad.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Understanding+National+Accounts&amp;rft.pages=18&amp;rft.pub=OECD&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.isbn=978-92-64-02566-0&amp;rft.aulast=Lequiller&amp;rft.aufirst=Fran%C3%A7ois&amp;rft.au=Derek+Blades&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DpXpJL6f8b3wC%26q%3D%2522To%2Bconvert%2BGDP%2Binto%2BGNI%2522&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGross+domestic+product" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<li id="cite&#95;note-94"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-94">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite class="citation web cs1"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://journals.hil.unb.ca/index.php/antistasis/article/viewFile/21119/24379">"<span class="cs1-kern-left"></span>"Bhutan GNH Index"<span class="cs1-kern-right"></span>"</a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20150212072510/http://journals.hil.unb.ca/index.php/antistasis/article/viewFile/21119/24379">Archived</a> from the original on 2015-02-12<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">2017-03-04</span></span>.</cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=%22Bhutan+GNH+Index%22&amp;rft&#95;id=http%3A%2F%2Fjournals.hil.unb.ca%2Findex.php%2Fantistasis%2Farticle%2FviewFile%2F21119%2F24379&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGross+domestic+product" class="Z3988"></span></span>

<p><b>Global</b>
</p>
<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.britannica.com/EBchecked/topic/246647">Gross domestic product</a> at the <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopædia Britannica</a></i></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.abs.gov.au/Ausstats/abs@.nsf/66f306f503e529a5ca25697e0017661f/3f880ee1d366198cca2569a400061616!OpenDocument">Australian Bureau of Statistics Manual on GDP measurement</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://data.oecd.org/gdp/gross-domestic-product-gdp.htm">OECD GDP chart</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090918013624/http://unstats.un.org/unsd/databases.htm">UN Statistical Databases</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://databank.worldbank.org/data/home.aspx">World Development Indicators (WDI)</a> at World Bank</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/publicdata/explore?ds=wb-wdi&amp;met=ny_gdp_mktp_cd&amp;idim=country:USA:CHN:FRA:DEU:JPN:GBR">World GDP Chart (since 1960)</a></li></ul>
<p><b>Data</b>
</p>
<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.bea.gov/national/index.htm#gdp">Bureau of Economic Analysis: Official United States GDP data</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.historicalstatistics.org/">Historicalstatistics.org: Links to historical statistics on GDP for countries and regions</a>, maintained by the Department of Economic History at <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_University" title="Stockholm University">Stockholm University</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.measuringworth.org/usgdp/">Historical U.S. GDP (yearly data)</a>, 1790–present, maintained by Samuel H. Williamson and Lawrence H. Officer, both professors of economics at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Illinois_at_Chicago" class="mw-redirect" title="University of Illinois at Chicago">University of Illinois at Chicago</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.rug.nl/ggdc/historicaldevelopment/maddison/releases/maddison-project-database-2020?lang=en">The Maddison Project</a> of the Groningen Growth and Development Centre at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Groningen" title="University of Groningen">University of Groningen</a>. This project extends the work of <a href="/wiki/Angus_Maddison" title="Angus Maddison">Angus Maddison</a> in collating all the available, credible data estimating GDP for countries around the world. This includes data for some countries for over 2,000 years and for all countries since 1950.</li></ul>
